0143119680|2.0|I read this book 3 months ago, and am now reading book 2. (So far, book 2 is pretty boring.)<br /><br />On to the review of A Discovery of Witches<br /><br />The first half of ADOW was tedious. Too much time spent explaining historical manuscripts and elaborating on food, wine, and clothing (black stretchy pants). This pedantic prose was exacerbated by the fact that I listened to the audio version and could not  skip ahead. The audio reader had a fine voice, nothing that distracted or annoyed, but the content was one big yawn much of the time.<br /><br />The story included some violence, one torture scene, and some relationship development. No love scenes, but some foreplay and suggested oral sex. Matthew called Diana his lion, in French, repeatedly. Repeatedly!!  (yet she was not so much a lion as a wet noodle).<br /><br />The characters were not so appealing. Diana the witch was helpless, obedient, whiny and dithering. She did not act like a 33 year-old with a Ph.D. She did not reflect the powerful ancestry that was hers, nor the upbringing with her aunt, a witch of strong character. I did not grow to love her.<br /><br />And Matthew the vampire did yoga and liked wine.  A lot of talk of yoga and wine. Matthew's character was bland, in my view.<br /><br />On the up side, the author inspired in me a desire to visit Oxford to explore the Bodleian Library.<br /><br />And even better, the pace picked up, FINALLY,  at around disk 14 (of 20). I cringed during the attack by the evil Satu, was charmed by the fascinating living witch-house and the adorable grandma ghost, and horrified by the attack of the jealous witch, Juliet. I liked getting to know Emma (Em) and Hamish and the daemon's pregnant wife, as secondary characters. Hope we see more of them.<br /><br />Despite the parts I did enjoy, I could not rate this more than 2 stars, based on the first 13 disks. If the book were shorter, or the pacing tighter, I could go with 3 or 4 stars.<br /><br />Another problem: Inconsistent character development. For example, given Matthew's strong alliance with the church from early childhood, the religious swear words seemed out of character. According to the story, Matthew prizes crucifixes, participated in the Crusades, was allied to the church and to the Knights Templar and/or Masons ( I forget) in his pre-vampire days, and generally was a devout Christian. So the profanity seemed jarring, given the context.  Further, it reflects a disregard for the diversity of readers, some of whom were brought up to avoid such language.<br /><br />For another example, given Diana's abilities, education, and heritage, she should not have been so bland, so boring, so obedient. Sometimes she seemed like wonder woman, but mostly not. What did I like? Seeing her develop her witchcraft.<br /><br />One more thing. The author seemed unclear of her genre. At times, it felt like I was reading historical fiction, due to all the info- dumps about old manuscripts and ancient societies,  but the book is classified as romance. I have nothing against historical fiction, and have read and loved McCullough's 1775, Sobel's Longitude, Shaara's Killer Angels, Cohen's Hinges of History series, etc.  Those authors know how to write captivating historical narrative! After romance, my next favorite genre is historical narrative.<br /><br />A few weeks ago, I heard Harkness speak at a local book store in the San Francisco area.  In the Q and A session, someone asked her what she had read of the paranormal genre, before embarking on her series. She said the only book with vampires she had read was by Ann Rice, and that she read it 20 years ago. In my view, this author needs to get a clue about this genre and the readership she is hoping to capture.nsistent character development. B001CC7PP8|5.0|Stephen Rebello's commentary is very informative but I am a little concerned at his nature of not backing up his conclusions with sources. There is an obvious mistake in it that further raises that concern. He states the doors used in the Victorian mansion were from the San Francisco Crocker Mansion. The Crocker mansion on Nob Hill was completely burned down in the 1906 earthquake, so that is obviously wrong. I am a historian of San Francisco Victorians and happen to own the doors used as props on the set that was built indoors and included the front porch and the interior room from the Psycho set. They were sold at auction by Butterfield's about 20 years ago and came with prop tags saying hold for Psycho IV. I have an acquaintance who owns the bedroom set from the mother's room. All of these props came from the 1872 Italianate Victorian mansion of California Senator Milton Latham call Linden Towers which was located in what is now called Menlo Park, just south of San Francisco on the peninsula. The house and furnishings cost $750,000 in 1872, not counting the 300 acres it sat on. The interiors were done by Herter Brothers of NYC. It was torn down during WWII when you could not buy lumber for sets due to rationing. Some of the big Hollywood studios bought most of the over-sized furniture (the house had 17 foot ceilings) and even the gasoliers, doors, and lumber at auction. These items were used in countless famous classic movies from that day forward. Warner Brothers also finally sold their cache of props from this house at auction about 3 years ago for huge sums of money.

While the images aren't quite as natural as you might like, there are very few distractions.  The colors are spectacular and the dancing is the greatest ever put on film.<br /><br />Gene Kelly seen here in his prime and in a tuxedo, could have been a great James Bond.  Cool, good looking with beautiful women all around.  Dancing never had a better stud.  Donald O'Connor is a second banana here, but check him out in his solo routine, \\"Make `Em Laugh\\".  He teams up with Kelly in a couple complicated numbers and you will never see better choreography.<br /><br />The story centers around the time when silent films made the transition to \\"talkies\\" (1927) and Kelly's leading lady couldn't make the jump due to her high pitched Brooklyneze voice.  Her voice and singing are later dubbed in by a newcomer named Kathy (Debbie Reynolds).  She's also Don's (Kelly) new fling.  This is all fun, inside stuff but the real action is on the dance floor.<br /><br />The film of course, features Kelly's take on the title song, which I've seen dozens of times.  It is still spectacular.  I had forgotten about the 13 minute dance sequence in the last act featuring Kelly and long-legged Cyd Charisse.  She dances throughout this number with a 30 foot long scarf flowing behind her.  It wraps gracefully around Kelly several times.  It is beautiful to watch, as is the whole movie.<br /><br />While the film in this Blu ray version is visually an upgrade from DVD, the audio is the real star.  The newest option is a lossless DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1 track.  It's a big deal.  The songs in particular have been given a wonderful mix.  The LFE channel is restrained keeping focus suggested by the original mono score.  Special features include a new documentary, commentary by several of the stars and others and a \\"jump-to-song\\" feature.  This is a pretty bare bones package (one disc) for what is a milestone film.new documentary, commentary by several of the stars and others and a \\"jump-to-song\\" feature. The same goes for the symphonies of Beethoven and Mozart.  I admit that the heavy hand of Bernstein, Bhm and von Karajan don't bear me out, but compare them with Mackerras and Krips, who both use a modern orchestra, but with a nobler hand.  My introduction to Josef Krips was in the late 50's when I got the complete Beethoven Piano Concerti for Christmas (Arthur Rubinstein was the pianist).  The first 3 concerti actually sound like music of the Classical Period; the 4th and 5th show the introduction of the Romantic Period.<br /><br />I love both the Pinnock and Marriner sets, but both set pale in comparison to the Krips Set on modern instruments.  I do however own the Symphonies 1 thru 20 by Marriner.  I realize my comments are my own and I speak for no one else.  I have no real criticism of either the Pinnnock or Marriner Set other than my preference here is the Krips Set.  I would recommend either of these 3 sets over any other that I have heard.  just remember that even the Mahler/Wagner conductors cannot totally ruin Mozart.  Beethoven and Mozart rise above even the worst readings.<br /><br />There appears to be some holes in the completeness of this set, but do your homework and you find that what is not there was probably not written my Mozart in the first place.  Whichever set you decide upon, get at least one and enjoy the divine spirituality of Mozart.  YOU DESERVE IT!!!

006197806X|3.0|Normally, I procrastinate a bit before writing a review.  I generally finish it and then come back in a few days to write the actual review.  With this book, though, I'll just plunge right into the critique.  Caution, there will be spoilers--including some allusions to the ending, though I won't spell it out entirely.<br /><br />First and foremost, I have to say that this book is so much better than Delirium that I could hardly believe I was reading the same series.  I've seen much praise of Lauren Oliver's writing, but I'd only read Delirium and wasn't very impressed by it, so I didn't quite get what the fuss was about.  Now, after reading this book, I do.  Oliver writes beautifully.  She has a way of exploring the depths of grief that I found so beautiful and melancholic without being overdone.  Lena felt like a much deeper, richer character in this book.  She showed so much growth over the course of the book, and Oliver wrote that growth in such a way that I was entirely convinced.  I finally understood why she had been so praised, and I wholeheartedly agreed with that praise.  Lauren Oliver can really write lovely prose without it coming across as schmaltzy.  Had this been the first book in the series, I'd have had a much different view of the series as a whole.<br /><br />Unfortunately, Oliver's beautiful prose isn't enough to save the book from what I thought was its biggest trap: its predictability.  The moment Lena laid eyes on Julian, I just knew *exactly* where Oliver was going with it.  While I did think their romance was convincing, and while I really liked the way Oliver showed the tension between Lena's gradually developing feelings for Julian and the pain it made her feel because of her memories of Alex, I just knew the two of them were going to end up together by the end of the book.  While Lena's character is well done in this book, I thought Julian's was trite, a plot device designed to teach the reader something about the flip side, and the requisite boy from the \\"wrong\\" side of the tracks with whom Lena learns to see eye to eye.  It pretty much bored me.  And I saw the ending of the book coming from approximately 3,000 miles away, and all I could do was groan in frustration and say, \\"Oliver, not you too!  There's no rule that says this HAS to be done in YA lit.  I don't care if everyone else is doing it, why did you have to go there too?\\"<br /><br />The other big problem I had with this book is that I still cannot buy the premise behind Oliver's dystopia.  However, I did think she did a better job of hinting at an agenda in which the whole idea of deliria would make sense.  Still, as good as the world-building was in this book, it still to me lacked legitimacy.  The structure is good and well drawn, but the foundation is really too shaky to support it.  I think Oliver is reaching for some big ideas here, but she's either just scratching the surface because she intends to bust them out in full force in the next book, or she didn't quite make it up the mountain.  Either way, it seriously impeded my enjoyment of this book.  As good as it was, I kept falling out of the narrative because there was literally a voice in my head saying, \\"This dystopia doesn't make any sense.\\"  It created a sort of cognitive dissonance in my brain because one part of me was thinking, \\"Wow, this book is so beautifully written and well done.  I love it!\\" while another part of me was thinking, \\"Yeah, but I still don't buy that this dystopia could exist because it doesn't make sense.\\"<br /><br />On the other hand, the world building was good.  I liked that Oliver added extra layers of complexity with the addition of the Scavengers, the people living in the subway, and the tension between the Pandemonium-style religious fundamentalists and their more measured counterparts.  All of these things expanded the world considerably, gave it some new shades of gray, and made it clear just how overwhelming Lena's situation is.  These new factionsalso had a lot to say about human nature and how motivated by self-interest people can be.  I also thought the Wilds were well done, and I was fascinated by the homesteads and by the way the members of the resistance on the inside communicated with those on the outside.  Oliver also added a lot of extra emotional resonance to the book with her descriptions of the bombings.  They gave a concrete sense of just how far those in power are willing to go to protect that power.<br /><br />In the end, what's good about this book is really good and what's bad is bad.  This has the effect of placing the book firmly in the three star rating because the bad features tended to cancel out the good features for me, leaving me with that sense of \\"this book's not bad\\", which is obviously not much of a ringing endorsement.  Still, it did pull me back into the series and I am planning on reading the Hana story along with the third installment, which is more than I can say for the way I felt after Delirium, when I decided I wasn't going to bother to read the rest of the series.  I'm really hoping that Oliver will pull it all together in the end because the bottom line is that she's a very good, very literary writer.  As much as I love YA fic, it's a genre that would only benefit from an infusion of writers with talent like Oliver's.

B000W7JWUA|4.0|This may come off as an expensive board game, but the value and entertainment you get from playing this easy-to-learn and highly social game is worth well beyond the $30 or so dollars you spend on it or the extra money put into expansions. Players have something to do almost every turn. There is a small element of luck with dice rolls, but strategy takes precedent 90% of the time. Trading is nothing like Monopoly and almost necessary to advance your building goals. Players are made to balance their choices turn by turn and make strategic builds and trades, but the concept and ease of play is remarkably great. For these reasons, definitely buy the game.<br /><br />However, for the price of the game you definitely expect the materials to be less flimsy. The sea tiles make setup a headache. From the start they were slightly warped. Getting them to interlock just so and stay that way is next to impossible. I count among my greatest Catan achievement just getting the board to stay set up perfectly through the whole game. One tile gets bumped which, consequentially, puts every other tile on the board askew. Presently, I am keeping the sea tiles under heavy books in an attempt to make them flat but 3 days in and no dice.<br /><br />Overall, I'm glad I bought the game for the fun times it gave my family and I, but cooperative and delicate board setup quenches the competitive mood pre-game.

It's thrilling, it's funny, the special effects look great, and the action feels real (no stiff, overly-armored Batman here!) and it reflects the appeal of comicbooks and comicbook culture better than any other adaptation I've seen.<br /><br />All praise is due to director Joss Whedon, who also co-wrote the script and (miraculously!) kept this from being one of those blechhy, top-heavy, written-by-Hollywood-committee monoliths that we've seen so far. The exposition is kept to a minimum, the lulls are lightened by classic Whedon humor, and the actors are treated like actors, not sort sort of squishy furniture you drape special effects around. Whedon, a guy that actors love, coaxes superior performances from Mark Ruffalo, Robert Downey Jr. and Scarlet Johansson, while Chris Evans' and Chris Hemsworth's presences are greatly enhanced from their solo debuts in \\"Captain America\\" and \\"Thor\\"; ditto for Thom Hiddleston as their nemesis, Loki, who was sort of okay in \\"Thor,\\" but great here. Samuel Jackson, intoning and pretentious in his role as Machiavellian uber-spy Nick Fury, drains the life out of every scene he's in, but that's sort of to be expected, and I'm willing to overlook it since the rest of the film is so awesome.<br /><br />I'm 1000% serious when I say Joss Whedon should be appointed the head of a studio: his time has come. He has almost single-handedly taken a genre that has been limping and misshapen for years, grievously misunderstood and poorly realized by the Hollywood machers, and made it into something magical and thrilling.<br /><br />He. Gets. It.<br /><br />Of course, if Whedon were put in charge, that might impede his ability to write and direct, but good god it would be great to have someone who can make something this good be in charge for a while. Warner should just appoint him the head of their superhero movie division for the next five-to-ten years and let him whip the genre into shape. All they need to do is make movies that are fun, and not so packed with dense exposition, and make 'em look good with strong, intelligent scripts. Whedon has done it here: this is the template. (DJ Joe Sixpack, Slipcue film reviews)nd direct, but good god it would be great to have someone who can make something this good be in charge for a while. 0525478817|5.0|The lingering tears that creased my eyes as I finished Hazel's story would not be sufficient proof for the effect Green's book had on me. I think he has managed to create that third space he writes about in the novel, a space where the reader and the book unite in a crescendo, where the words no longer seek to be read, but to be honored and to be remembered for infinity. 'The Fault in our Stars' is not just about two cancer-ridden teenagers, their angst and their motivations for fighting this distraught battle. I found it to be a poignant and utterly tacit work on what all of us seek, what we get, and what we don't. How we fight our battles and what those battles truly are. Being encumbered by a disease can incite a radical notion, a strong desire to leave a mark that Augustus so badly wanted to leave, so that oblivion would not grasp him and leave him breathless. But Green uses cancer not so much as a tool to make us appreciate his characters more, but to allow us to remember that we are all a part of this juggernaut of life, and that this was a story he wished would make love, friendship and all the 'metaphors' of life seem relevant again. And it did. For all its worth Gus, I will follow the risen sun, and see the light. Take a bite off this book. Maybe you will find yourself afflicted with love as I did.

0812993292|5.0|When I heard Rachel Joyce interviewed by Diane Rehm on National Public Radio, I thought I'd just \\"give this novel a go.\\" (Do I sound a wee bit British there?) And when I began my journey into it--my reading pilgrimage albeit not exactly an unlikely one--I knew immediately I would have to make myself read at the pace of Harold Fry's walk. Slow. He suffered blisters. I suffered nothing except the annoyance of telling myself that this was like a box of chocolates--no, I am not about to Forrest Gump you: enjoy the taste of one at a time, one chapter at a time because otherwise I would be far too quickly done. Some novels are to be savored. And this is certainly one of those.<br /><br />This novel is, as the Brits say, \\"brilliant! Just brilliant!\\" Harold has been married to Maureen for over forty years, has recently retired from a job he disliked in most respects with a truly horrific boss. And one day \\"the post\\" delivers a pink envelop addressed to Harold and in it a short note from Queenie saying she has been diagnosed with cancer and is now in hospice care. He worked with Queenie. She is in Berwick-upon-Tweed which, according to the map at the beginning of the novel, is just over the English border with Scotland. Harold lives in Kingsbridge. No, I too knew not of the location of either. But there it is on the map, just as far away from Queenie as he could possibly be and still be within the confines of the landmass the houses England, Wales and Scotland, right down on the English Channel.<br /><br />Harold writes a short note and wearing his yachting shoes--canvas ones I assume, probably what we call Dockers--makes his way to the nearest postslot, stopping to chat for a moment with his neighbor, now a widower. But on his way--mind you, he has nothing but what he walked out of the house with--he calls the hospice and tells the person on the other end to tell Queenie to stay as well as she can, that he is on his way. He has no luggage, but he calls Maureen to tell her. She, of course, is astonished. He has no map, no idea of how to get there other than to head north. He has led a rather provincial life.<br /><br />Maureen is, as an American friend of mine would say, \\"a piece of work.\\" She reminds me somewhat of Hyacinth Bucket (Patricia Rutledge) in the BBC sitcom \\"Keeping Up Appearances\\" but without the outrageous attire \\"Mrs. Bouquet\\" wears.<br /><br />So along the way--and the reader can keep track of the slowness of the journey by checking out the map--we are provided with back stories, about Harold's failures as a husband and father to only child David--he is quite the mystery for much of the novel--and of Harold's disengaged parents we well as Maureen's, about the lack of any type of relationship they now have. And about Queenie. But, of course, Harold meets a lot of people along the way, staying in inns and B&Bs. One couple, identically dressed for hiking, seem like the perfect match until the husband opens his mouth: full of advice, especially for what Harold should be wearing. \\"It occurred to Harold that he [the hiker] was one of those people who didn't require other people in order to have a conversation.\\" And then this from his wife: \\"'Personally I liked the Cotswolds [the husband had said they did not].' said his wife. 'I know it's more flat, but it's romantic.' She twiddle her wedding ring so hard it looked as if she might unscrew her finger.\\" (page 73) And this when Harold arrives in Exeter and visits a cathedral: \\"It struck him as strange but true that tourists bought trinkets and souvenirs of religious places because they had no idea what else to do when they got there.\\" (page 84) That is just a very small taste of the brilliant little pieces of this very brilliant 300 page novel.<br />Then there comes a real surprise near the end although all the pieces had been laid out for that short chapter, a letter to the girl who inspired Harold to go on his pilgrimage<br /><br />This is a coming-of-age story, but not one ofa young person stepping from innocence into life's realities. Instead it is a journey--an \\"unlikely pilgrimage\\"--in which a 65-year-old man who's lived a rather isolated and not very happy life walks into a broader world: \\"He understood that in walking to atone for the mistakes he had made, it was also his journey to accept the strangeness of others.\\" Amen!a young person stepping from innocence into life's realities. There is a sense of anxiety and fear in the song that is rare to see in music that isn't metal. Some of the lyrics feel like they were pulled out of nowhere and don't seen to fit, but it's a good way to end the album... except there's one more.<br /><br />12. Slim Pickens Does The Right Thing And Puts A Very Long Title On This Song - 9/10<br />It seems to switch between disgust and anger and despair repeatedly throughout the song, creating three distinct sections to the finale song. 145162445X|3.0|A Universe from Nothing, A Hit and a Miss<br /><br />Lawrence Krauss's book A Universe from Nothing, presents a wealth of knowledge and a dearth of wisdom.  The book calls to mind the Chinese invasion of Tibet.  In To a Mountain in Tibet, writer Colin Thubron observes, \\"Where the Tibetans sense spirit the Chinese see superstition.\\"  In their invasion of Tibet, the Chinese sought not only to establish a new regime but also to eradicate a state of religious consciousness.  Tibet would come under the control of Chinese authority and Tibet would be swept clean of what the Chinese deemed messy and distasteful habits of mind.  Krauss's book has a similar dual purpose:  he intends to clarify our understanding of the universe, and he attempts to cleanse the world of the influence of philosophers and theologians.  With the righteousness of any rabid religious or political fundamentalist, Krauss boasts that \\"theology has made no contribution to knowledge in the past five hundred years at least\\" (144).  His writing about the physics of the universe is edifying, but his superficial assessment of non-materialistic thinking undermines the book's overall success.<br /><br />The book presents a clear and exciting review of the intellectual history of the fields of particle physics and cosmology that bear on the something-from-nothing mystery (Krause calls it a \\"conundrum\\").  Krauss's scientific narrative is compelling--a 4 star writing performance.  He is a fine storyteller.  His argument is convincing when the nothing he describes is empty space and when he claims that quantum theory allows for the emergence of matter from \\"the energy of empty space.\\" More speculatively, he suggests answers to the question of \\"a more fundamental nothingness from which empty space may have arisen, preexisted, and is eternal.\\"  His most plausible theory here is the multiverse, a condition without beginning or end or comprehensibility, a condition in which anything is possible except a god or a wisetheologian.  In short, he presents an explanation for existence not incompatible with the science of our day, but an existence not as mysterious, rich, or relevant as the one he wants to destroy for the rest of us.<br /><br />In his desire to eradicate the world of spirit Krause displays a disturbing lack of spiritual literacy.  He ignores the actual language of relevant theologians/philosophers/mystics; he also ignores any reports by ordinary folk with extraordinary nonmaterial experiences since, apparently, such considerations \\"muddy the waters.\\" (xvi)  He is not on speaking terms with the non-materialist culture he condemns.  Most of us, however, probably live partly in the world of the spirit, our own imaginative Tibet, where reports of the inspired, the mad, and the gifted are allowed to be taken seriously.  In this place we can entertain the possibilities of life beyond death as suggested by near-death experiences, as well as precognition, reincarnation, healings, miracles, prophetic dreams, mystical unions, and on and on.  We may attribute most of these reports to delusion or wishful thinking or fraud, but an open mind will find many of them enriching and convincing.  Krauss will have none of it.  He clearly needs to heed his own advice:  \\"A truly open mind means forcing our imagination to conform to the evidence of reality, and not vice versa, whether or not we like the implication.\\" (139). The traditions of religious, philosophical, and spiritual insights, as well as the lived experience of the common folk are evidence of a non-material reality that Krauss should not dismiss without a hearing.<br /><br />Moreover, when life pitches a knuckleball most of us don't turn to the pages of a Newton or a Dawkins or a Krauss.  When real trouble strikes, discoveries and theories about the material world do not sustain and instruct the soul.  At this fundamental level of struggling to understand the reality of the universe, we turn to the non-material wisdom of a Gandhi, a Buber, a Whitehead, a King, a Mother Teresa, a Bonhoeffer.  When Krauss proclaims that \\"philosophy and theology are ultimately incapable of addressing by themselves the truly fundamental questions that perplex us about our existence\\" (178), one can only wonder how someone so intelligent can be so narrow in his own philosophy.
1606995359|5.0|Volume 2 (actually, Volume 12, going by publisher order) of the Fantagraphics Carl Barks library reprints the first six issues of UNCLE $CROOGE: three FOUR COLOR features and U$ 4, 5, and 6.  These are the legendary tales and gags that transformed Scrooge McDuck from an intriguing supporting player in Barks' DONALD DUCK stories and \\"ten-pagers\\" into comicdom's great champion of high adventure and low (yet surprisingly amusing) cheapness.  There's no need for me to add to the piles of praise that have been justifiably heaped upon these efforts, except to note that Fantagraphics' production values easily live up to the high standards set in the first released volume of this series.  In this case, that even includes posing a stiff challenge to Duck fans' eyeballs and reproducing the exact same coloring schemes from the original Dell comics.  Did the color of Scrooge's broadcloth coat really travel the precipitous route from red-with-green-trim to green to plum to red to blue over such a short span of time -- sometimes changing from feature to feature within a single issue?  Did the Beagle Boys really have blue sweaters in \\"Only a Poor Old Man\\" before switching over to their trademark red/orange?  Yes, and FG faithfully depicts every single twist and turn.<br /><br />I was a little disappointed to see that George Lucas' foreword tribute to Barks is \\"merely\\" a reprint of the piece he wrote for the Celestial Arts book in the early 1980s.  C'mon, George... to cadge a metaphor from \\"Only a Poor Old Man,\\" a lot of water has crashed through the dam since then.  Don't you have any updated insights to provide to us, on the order of those you imparted to eager fans during your triumphant revisiting of the Star Wars \\"universe\\"?  (Or perhaps I should be careful what I wish for.)  The Story Notes continue to be quite good, though I can certainly see the point made by other reviewers who wish that the Notes for a particular story could precede that story.  It is a little awkward tohave to flip back to the rear of the book to read the Notes.  That being said, the Notes section also presents visual, as well as textual, information, such as story panels, covers, and, in the case of \\"Back to the Klondike,\\" some redrawn \\"lost\\" art (which is incorporated quite magnificently into the reconstructed original tale, I might add).  It is difficult to see how all of this could be broken up and presented on a story-by-story basis without making the job of reading even more confusing and wrecking what is presumably one of FG's aims, namely, to give the reader something as close to the original Dell-comic reading experience as possible.  Perhaps FG should consider putting a discreet footnote at the bottom of the title page of each story, indicating where the Notes for that story can be found in the back of the volume.  The purists may not like the move, but it may serve to satisfy more people.

Then start the driver software and THEN turn on the headset. This worked for me. For a film in the horror genre, \\"The Wicker Man\\" comes very close to being an integrated musical, but does so with (largely) subtle wit and without undercutting the growing sense of menace which is quite a trick. The music credit for move goes to \\"Magnet\\" which may be an obscure celtic group which is capable of throwing in a little psychedelic guitar as needed or Magnet may be a collective name for some studio magicians and a few of the celtic musicians who appear on camera in the film; either way the music itself is catchy and evocative the region and the people it presents, but the way the music is incorporated produces an interesting hybrid, a film that is not quite a musical horror movie but something not far removed from that either. Take a look at the scene titled \\"Siren's Song\\" in the scenes index, which features Britt Ekland as a character named Willow singing and tapping on the wall of the room in which Edward Woodward's sexually repressed police sergeant is attempting to sleep and you certainly can't help but note how erotic the scene is but you may not stop to consider how odd it is to see a scene in a horror movie that would fit right into a film with Bob Fosse choreography. The second time you watch \\"The Wicker Man\\" (and if you watch it once the odds are very high that you will watch it again) take note of the abundance of singing and dancing and how well it is melded into the film.

B000AP04MO|5.0|I saw \\"Two for the Road\\" in a theater at Ford Ord, California in April 1967. The price of admission was 50 cents, double the usual 25-cent price, because it was a first-run feature. When a soldier making $90 a month faces a markup like that, it had better be a great film.<br /><br />In retrospect, it would have been worth today's $14 (or more) ticket price. Those 111 minutes have remained with me for 45 years.<br /><br />This film was the first I had ever seen with a non-linear narrative, and it was a different and fascinating experience to see how the film makers blended the various stages of the relationship between Joanna (Audrey Hepburn) and Mark (Albert Finney). I can just imagine the effort and time involved in film editing to create the clever transitions among a half-dozen time periods, employing dialogue, clothing and hair styles to seamlessly weave this story of a stormy-yet-passionate marriage.<br /><br />As the film neared its conclusion on that April evening, I wondered if anyone else in the theater was enjoying the film as much as I. After all, we were soldiers, mostly in our late teens or early 20s, perhaps more inclined to appreciate a \\"Beach Blanket Bingo\\" or \\"Dirty Dozen\\"-type movie than this sophisticated, stylish and complex film. But it appears \\"Two For The Road\\" struck a chord in every one in the audience. The entire theater broke into applause at the closing credits and everyone from my group of buddies talked about for days after the screening. It is a credit to director Stanley Donen, screenwriter Frederic Raphael and the cast, that this remarkable movie still remains one of my favorites.

0143039881|5.0|Before we got to know her as a philosopher, or as a legal and an academic scholar, Hanna Arendt was a journalist for the New Yorker magazine. It was in this capacity as an eye witness to the trial that she accumulated material for this book, which first appeared as a series of articles in that magazine a year or so after the trial.<br /><br />As usual, Professor Arendt's supple mind and precise writings are on exquisite display here. In her other roles, she is well known for not suffering fools lightly, and here too she does not stoop or even give a nod or a hint to political correctness, or cheap but anticipated ethnic pandering. Apparently trying to maintain her high standards of scholarship is what got her in trouble regarding certain aspects of this book. Yet, despite the hoopla, in my view, she remains the \\"real deal,\\" raising our IQs as she teaches us how to think correctly about complex, sensitive and raw emotional issues like race, ethnicity, anti-Semitism and the kidnapping and the trial of Adolph Eichmann, all issues that I thought I already knew all there was to know?<br /><br />During 1961, Eichmann's capture and kidnapping from a suburb near Sao Paulo, Brazil was a sensation. We all predicted the script of the trial: that Eichmann would quickly be found guilt and hanged.  And although that was indeed exactly how it turned out, it takes the acute eye and a mind like that of Dr. Arendt to give us all of the textured and nuanced layers that do not meet the casual and legally untrained eye. That is where the richness of this book shines through.  Every reader must be grateful to her for pointing out all the non-obvious aspects of what we all saw as a cut-and-dried trial.<br /><br />For instance, it never occurred to me at the time (or even later), that Prime Minister Ben-Gurion, the architect of Israeli statehood, was also the director of the stage play that would become the Eichmann trial. The trial was clearly Israeli Kabuki theater but also it was globaltheater too. According to the author, one would not have been incorrect to see the trial as Mr. Ben-Gurion's own personally orchestrated \\"show trial.\\" Ben-Gurion after all believed that sitting in judgment of Eichmann was the business of Jews alone since Eichmann was an enemy of the Jewish people.<br /><br />Under Ben-Gurion's theatrical directorship, the trial thus was turned into a pure \\"show trial,\\" a symbolic and didactic exercise, a forum for teaching Israelis that Israel remained the only true safe-haven for them from an anti-Semitic world gone mad; for teaching Christians that Eichmann, the ovens, and the gas chambers were the logical end points of 1500 years of Christian directed hatred towards the Jews; to teach the world about Jewish history and about the continuous suffering of the Jewish people; and finally the trial was a drama about the perpetrators, the Nazis. It gave them names and addresses and shamed the culture from which they came, just as it catalogued what they did, to whom they had done it, why they did it, and how they had done it. In short, Eichmann became the \\"stand-in\\" for all of the monsters that Nazis and Christian culture had produced.<br /><br />Yet, Ben-Gurion's agenda was not even half the story of the meaning of the Eichmann trial. In addition to Ben-Gurion's agenda, Eichmann's trial was also about a host of other important issues -- things that only a mind like Dr. Arendt's could conger up, uncover and then explain. Here is a short list of them to end this review:<br /><br />First, Eichmann was on trial for his murderous deeds only -- for which the prosecution could not produce a single witness who saw Eichmann kill anyone? The best they could produce is a hearsay conversation in which Eichmann was supposed to have suggested in a coded way, having certain people killed. He was not on trial for the suffering of the Jewish people. He was not even on trial for his anti-Semitism or racist views. For if he had been, then the Israeli state, one of the world's only race-based states, would also have to answer for having Nazi-like laws against mixed marriages, and its way of declaring all mixed-race babies as bastards, etc.<br /><br />Second, as this last point makes clear, whether it wanted to be or not, the Israeli state, its ways of justice, its culture, and even its way of life were also on trial. In fact it was in this area where the author, a Jew herself, got into big trouble. She correctly insinuates that \\"show trials\\" are usually not about justice but are about larger political issues beyond the courtroom. While the Israeli court that hanged Eichmann failed to show that he had personally killed anyone, it did prove what Eichmann had readily admitted to: that he was but a cog in a cultural and bureaucratic wheel whose hub was Nazism, which revolved around anti-Semitism and a vast machinery of death.<br /><br />But being true to the racist social order in which he found himself did not make Eichmann a criminal in the eyes of the German laws he was duty bound to obey and uphold. Indeed he was a murderer for the state in the same that an ordinary soldier is. Is this not true of patriots in most countries, including the USA? Because of his role as a German patriot, Eichmann's conscience was no more troubled when he killed Jews than when any soldier's is who kills a perceived enemy of his state? Nor did he violate any known German laws by following Hitler's directives, directives which had the same status and effect as law.<br /><br />It is in this vast area of the morality of cultures, the morality of state laws, and the morality of people under extreme duress within them that Dr. Arendt earns her pay. She raises and then tries to answer two forbidden holocaust questions, both of which got her into trouble with Jews. The first one was: \\"Why did Jews go meekly to the gas chambers without protest, even when it was obvious that there were times when they could have easily overpowered the guards? (whom they always outnumbered, and as eventually happened at Sobibor)<br /><br />Her two-part answer was the best I have heard so far: The first part is profound and with the unmistakable ring of truth. As she puts it: \\"the triumph of the SS, demands that the tortured victim allow himself to be led to the noose without protesting, that he renounce and abandon himself to the point of ceasing to affirm his identity.\\" The second part of the answer is equally profound and even more succinct: It is that there are some punishments much worse than death itself and all them were held in abeyance: reserved for Jews collectively in the event of uprisings and rebellions.<br /><br />The second question had to do with the morality of the Judenrate, Jews who ran the ghettos for the Nazis and thus who collaborated with them, selecting and sending many of their own Jewish neighbors to be gassed -- ostensibly so as to be able to either save others or delay their execution. As I understand her argument in this instance, there comes a time when humans under extreme stress and duress, that see morality as an abstraction, must give way to the reality staring them in the face? The author argues that this was the case for the Judenrate whose very existence was balanced on a slender thread of Nazi capriciousness. Their decisions were not ones made strictly on the basis of abstract moral principles alone, that is to say, only on the abstract moral plane, but were made on the plane of individual pragmatism, on their own  \\"Realpolitik\\" of the moment.<br /><br />The upshot of the book is that Eichmann was neither especially evil or immoral but a colorless vanilla cog in the wheel of a bureaucracy based on racial superiority mass murder and genocide. And as we have seen, such governments did not whither away with the defeat of the Nazis: they still stalk the post-modern world. Five Stars

B006VKKAWQ|4.0|I am not a big fan of Arturo Toscanini (certainly not an unconditional admirer), but as man and musician he was undeniably important. Apart from his importance in terms of influence and popularity, he did set some standards that are worth maintaining. His fixation on precision could get wearisome, but orchestral standards have risen, partly due to him, partly for many other reasons. There is an high enough proportion of great, fine, or interesting recorded performances in Toscanini`s output to make having a complete set of his R.C.A. Victor studio recordings, available together in a single package like this one, a most desirable acquisition for one`s collection.<br /><br />Most of all, however, Toscanini`s greatness, for me, lies in how superbly well he conducted and interpreted the repertory of composers for whom he had the most affinity. First of all, LUIGI CHERUBINI. Toscanini did much to kick-start the revival of interest in Cherubini`s music that, fortunately, has continued. Toscanini`s recordings, however, do suffer from the sound in which they were captured. Cherubini`s music is beautifully orchestrated, so the potential charm of hearing such fine interpretations of Cherubini`s lone symphony and of the overtures to his operas which Toscanini chose to record (and to conduct publicly frequently in his concerts and broadcasts) is limited by the pale sound, which does so much to undermine these recordings. As for the great Requiem in C Minor, the choral-orchestral balances are even more damaging, the cunningly orchestrated accompaniment at times all but inaudible when the chorus is singing (that is, most of the time). Nonethess, Toscanini was a giant in this repertory!<br /><br />For me, the other composers whose music Toscanini best conducted were, above all, Wagner (wonderfully vigourous and enlivening!), Berlioz, Verdi (toweringly great) and numerous other Italians (e.g., Cimarosa, Rossini, Boito, Wolf-Ferrari, Puccini, Catalani, Respighi, et alia), who music he directed exceedingly well. As for the Viennese classics, although Toscanini, intermittently, made some truly wonderful recordings of certain of Beethoven`s symphonies (nos. 3, 7, and 9), of the overtures, and of other works (instrumental or vocal), too many of his performances of this sublime composer are just too hectic to do Beethoven`s music justice. Then there are the other Viennese masters, i.e. Haydn, Mozart, and Schubert, and Toscanini`s way with their music is, variously, just too brutal, uncomprehending, or just plain botched to take seriously. Of course, for that other towering figure active in Vienna later in the 19th century, Brahms, Toscanini did very well, indeed.<br /><br />The above is generalising, and there are many other individual composers and performances among Toscanini`s output of studio recordings that deserve some mention (conducting the music of Gluck, Schumann, Elgar, Richard Strauss, and of many other composers), but I am not enough of an advocate for this conductor to take the effort and space to go on at length to deal with them. There are some more exhaustive reviews on Amazon, especially on Amazon`s U. S. of A. WWW site, for those who desire such a level of discussion of this compendious release. Nor do I care to go into detail regarding the various permutations, analogue and digital, of Toscanini reisuues, though I would say that the various segments of the B.M.G. Classics numbered series, \\"Arturo Toscanini, the Immortal\\", is about as good as I have heard among the various CD reissues of his output, albeit purchasing some of these other rengineered complete CD editions can cost  considerably more than the one under review here. Toscanini`s way with music, at its best, is so superb that it is worth having a comprehensive set such this one, even if it means that some of the individual CDs within it may get little play, at least \\"chez moi\\".<br /><br />Acquire this, if you can!he directed exceedingly well. 0307730697|4.0|It took me a great deal of deliberate thought to decide how to rate and review this book. It has limited amounts of profanity, as well as some unnecessary and vulgar accounts of incidents.  Some reviewers have said that they enjoyed this for adding a sense of realism to the book.  I actually disagree and feel like the curses and vulgar accounts were not necessary, and detracted from an otherwise great telling of this man's life.  I was especially surprised when I came across these items because I was aware that it was from a Christian publisher.  At any rate, said occurrences were limited in amount and were definitely overshadowed by the legacy this man left behind and is recounted in the book.<br /><br />What I really loved about this book was the way that it presented so many aspects of who Adam Brown was.  He wasn't just a marine - he was a committed Christian, devoted husband, loving father, faithful servant, heroic leader, and loyal friend.  I felt like the author opened up a door for us to get inside  Adam's head and heart to see the complexity of a man who leads with a heart of valor, while internally struggling with addiction beyond the understanding of many Christians, myself included.  While at times the image presented seems almost super-human, the author did a great job bringing out the fun-loving, simple-living man that Adam Brown was.  He did some amazing things, but at the end of the day, he was still flesh and bone and the kind of guy anyone would want to hang out with. A spiritual testimony for a Christian is given out of desire for others to hear the gospel proclaimed through his or her own story, and I think Adam would have wished this be included in his story.  Considering that the author is not a Christian, I feel he did a good job of evidencing the strong change in Adam as a result of God's work in his life and it likely didn't occur to the author that the presentation of the gospel is such a crucial aspect of a Christian's spiritual testimony.<br /><br />Overall, I would recommend this book to a limited audience of mature readers.<br /><br />Disclaimer: I received a free copy of this book from the publisher in return for an honest review.<br /><br />Mrs. Renninger

In the past, the story of the Tuskegee airmen usually revolves around the early days of the struggle to be recognized and accepted. This film does that, especially in the accompanying documentary.  However the film also depicts the actual wartime experiences of these brave pioneers of African-American civil rights. The aerial sequences are astoundingly good as is the portrayal of aerial combat. The war in Italy in 1944 is realistically depicted. So is Army Air Force pilot life. People interested in World War II military history and USAF vets (I am one) will treasure the depiction of the men and aircraft in this film. You will wonder how the producers managed to get so many working WWII planes and you will struggle to tell the difference between the CGI aircraft and the real ones. The only sour note in this film is the cliche ridden dialogue and inane chatter between the pilots. However I have been around real real military aircrews to know that some of these verbal groaners actually do occur, but to place so many in a motion picture takes the viewers attention away from the story.<br /><br />Watch this film for the excellent photography and ignore the cornball script.

0805095535|4.0|Paul Auster's [[ASIN:0805095535 Winter Journal]] is a compelling review of his life up to age 64.  Very well written, though rather oddly done in the 2nd person (perhaps because he sees his body as outside of himself?).  I read it in two sittings on a single day, unwilling to stop even though little in the book is really compelling.  It should perhaps be titled \\"Autumn Journal\\" as it ends \\"You have entered the winter of your life.\\", which seems an accurate description of what life is like at age 64.<br /><br />There's a fascinating rhythm to the language in the book, like a fast walk through the streets of Manhattan.  This is quite deliberate as the author discusses writing as a lesser form of dance, based on the rhythms of walking.  Such writing is a pleasure and there are a number of these pleasures throughout the book.<br /><br />I found two sections of the memoir to be especially good.  His description of his mother's death is quite moving.  And oddly enough, his summary of the plot to the 1950 movie \\"D.O.A.\\" somehow becomes both personal and powerful.<br /><br />I suspect that this memoir would be of less interest to younger readers, but for this late middle-aged reader it was completely absorbing and provided some (cold) comfort in knowing that we all face similar problems of body, memory, and meaning as we approach the winter of our lives.

As a BORN AGAIN Doors fan (as in, I was a fan as a kid and recently 're-discovered' them), I felt a moral obligation to warn people against this ATROCITY!  If it were possible, I would DESTROY all available copies to protect the INNOCENT and UNASSUMING!    First, the title, \\"THE DOORS,\\" is MISLEADING.  Instead, it should have been called \\"MORRISON.\\"  Stone sees Morrison as THE central figure in The Doors saga, with the OTHERS being merely BACK-UP players.  NOTHING could have been FURTHER from the TRUTH.  Morrison himself would've attested to that.  As proof, the band shared songwriting credits on most songs, attesting to the true COLLABORATIVE nature of this band.    Second, EVERYONE, including Morrison, comes off a a TWO dimensional character in this film.  John Densmore (horribly portrayed by one-note actor Kevin Dillon) is ALWAYS sulking.  Ray Manzarek comes across SO straight-laced (?) as to practically be a MARTINET, and poor Pamela Courson, as played by Meg Ryan, comes across as some STUPID hippy-dippy chick.    Third, Kilmer's perfomance as Morrison is SO overrated.  He portrays Morrison as this VACUOUS, SELF-CENTERED ego maniac, as opposed to the flesh and blood artist that he was, full of CONTRADICTIONS and COMPLEXITIES.    Fourth, Stone uses Morrison's ACTUAL song lyrics in place of dialogue, which comes across as BAD as it sounds, while the remaining dialogue is as CHEESY as it gets (i.e.: ROBBY KRIEGER: \\"All I know is, I played with Dionysius.\\")  Oh, puh-leeze!!    For both old fans/new fans, and those curious to learn about the phenomena that was THE DOORS, I HIGHLY recommend Tom DiCillo's \\"WHEN YOU'RE STRANGE.\\"  It's there that you'll discover that The Doors were a BAND, not \\"Jim Morrison and...\\"  You'll also discover that there reallywas no NEED for anyone to PLAY \\"Jim Morrison\\".  Jim Morrison was \\"playing\\" him all along, and NO ONE could have played him better.  It's like asking someone to play CARY GRANT!    If a MOVIE had to be made about The Doors, ANY filmmaker could've mined (and STILL can) the RICH territory of what happens when a band achieves MASSIVE success through a hit single like \\"LIGHT MY FIRE,\\" and thus become teeny-bopper idols, when they'd rather explore the UNKNOWN through songs like \\"THE END\\" and be taken seriously by a more MATURE audience.  Now THAT would've made a film even former film student Jim Morrison would enjoyed!    p.s.:  for examples of rock bio films that have done it RIGHT, I submit the following for those LUCKY enough NOT to have seen them yet. LA BAMBA (1987) - Corny, but EFFECTIVE, film about pioneering rock star Ritchie Valens.  Cliched?  yes, but damn, it works! BACKBEAT (1994) - BEST film about the Beatles yet.  Not as UNTOUCHABLE, PERFECT rock gods, but as AMBITIOUS, DETERMINED young men, doing what they have to do to grab that GOLDEN ring. B005LAII3U|3.0|Remaking a classic film (even if it's only a cult classic) can be a strange business: if it isn't broke, why fix it?  That's the logic that applies to the newest iteration of Arnold Schwarzenegger's sci-fi classic, \\"Total Recall.\\"<br /><br />During the twenty second century in the aftermath of a devastating chemical war, the two remaining nations on earth exist in an uneasy state of underground warfare and terrorism.  Among it all, factory worker Douglas Quaid seeks to find meaning in his boring, tedious life.  Desiring to obtain some artificial memories of a more exciting life, Quaid quickly finds himself thrust into a world of war, intrigue, and where nothing is as it seems.<br /><br />What really sets this modern \\"Recall\\" apart from the original is that it features a more serious, gritty approach to the nearly-identical storyline (which means no witty one liners, trips to Mars, or mutated belly-aliens).  And yet, that's not a bad thing, as it makes for a unique telling of the story, complete with a few welcome changes (Quaid's wife gets a much bigger role, and I found the idea of two continents connected by a massive elevator very interesting).  The production design is also fantastic, combining the visual look of Minority report, the Fifth Element, and Blade Runner, with lots of futuristic tech filling the screen, and delighting the eyes.<br /><br />Still, for all its visual powers, \\"Recall\\" doesn't feel like it has much weight to it: it's mainly an action movie, with the fascinating \\"Who am I?\\" plot playing second fiddle.  Most of the film is devoted to seemingly endless chase scenes among futuristic grey buildings and streets, with almost no humor or moments of levity, which gives the impression that the film takes itself a little too seriously.<br /><br />In the end, \\"Total Recall\\" is a good example of a middle of the road remake.  It does distinguish itself from what came before with a more serious tone, superb visuals, and a very creative climax.  While nota classic, it's serviceable, and an interesting take on the original classic.

B008645YEE|4.0|\\"Here you see the essence of Hans' approach,\\" writes director Chris Nolan in the liner notes of this CD. \\"He is not playing along...Hans sees through the screen to the dark beating heart of the story.\\" The whole note is worth reading, about what Hans Zimmer has contributed to Nolan's groundbreaking vision of the Batman story, and specifically to this final film: 'The Dark Knight Rises.'<br /><br />And Nolan is right. From the beginning the Batman scores have eschewed the bombastic themes of most superhero movies and stuck with the idea of the Batman - the absence of theme, expressing instead the character itself, the dark undercurrent that creeps under everything, swelling and fading and - in this story - rising to meet the ultimate evil.<br /><br />Zimmer's score for 'The Dark Knight Rises' is almost everything you could hope for, flowing naturally from the familiar themes of the first two films into uncharted territory with new threats and new characters. The menace of Bane expressed in raw, primal rhythms, the bright dance of the cat burglar, the subtle notes of introspection, the progression from the dark, rising to the light. It's not easy to pick a favorite song - while these themes pervade the entire score, \\"Imagine the Fire\\" somehow captures them all in a single sequence, rising and falling, bright strings and edgy synth and pounding drum. This song alone is worth the price of admission for this score.<br /><br />And despite how good it is, how well it meets the challenge the movie presents, Zimmer's score for 'The Dark Knight Rises' is missing something. It's missing the balance of James Newton Howard's fine touch. While it's probably accurate to say that Howard's contribution was less than Zimmer's in the previous two movies, its absence is felt here. 'Dark Knight Rises' is all Zimmer, and you can hear it - there's hints of 'Black Rain' here, and echoes of 'Gladiator'. Missing, though, is the subtlety and balance of Howard's touch, and after the first two masterpieces, I can't help but think this would have been an even better score with him included.<br /><br />That being said, I still love it. It's a perfect match for the movie and it's delightful to listen to in its own right. If you're in any doubt, just take a few minutes and give \\"Imagine the Fire\\" a test drive - listen to the dark beating heart of the story and let it rise, rise, rise.masterpieces, I can't help but think this would have been an even better score with him included.<br /><br />That being said, I still love it. It's a perfect match for the movie and it's delightful to listen to in its own right. If you're in any doubt, just take a few minutes and give \\"Imagine the Fire\\" a test drive - listen to the dark beating heart of the story and let it rise, rise, rise.

But if yours were pressed in the US, I got bad news for you... they sound inferior, possibly due to a copy of the original master being used to make them. That affects the singles as well: if UK - keep, if US - dispose.<br /><br />Alright, I hope I made my points clear: buy the set, enjoy the rarities, sell the studio albums (except Think Tank), keep UK-released Blur stuff you had before. For my standard Windows 7 application usage, I am very impressed. My Windows Experience Index is in the 6-7 range, and all of the programs that I run function flawlessly. From a standard productivity viewpoint, I have a very powerful PC at a very low cost. I have not noticed any noise coming from the CPU fan - certainly nowhere near the level of noise that comes from the AMD-81xx CPU's I've owned.<br /><br />As regards productivity I have had no issues with APU performance. There's some Turkish distribution of Linux that supports the Llano APU's because they aren't held to the same copyright / IP laws as the distributions coming from the European or American West. I'm just a little upset at this because for my uses a Linux system would be much more practical.<br /><br />Overall this is a good processor - 65W is a very low power draw, meaning this product runs very cool and utilizes very little electricity for a desktop system. No need for a fancy water-cooled CPU heatsink. If you want to strike a balance between power usage and performance this is an excellent platform. And really, I have no complaints at all using this as a Windows 7 processor. But if I were to start gaming and using some really intensive encoding software, I'd probably go i7 with discrete video and audio.<br /><br />Update as of September 20th, 2012 - a Note to Linux Users<br /><br />After initial frustrations attempting to load various fglrx files and amd-cccle on Linux distributions that proved non-supportive of the integrated graphics capability of this card, I went as far as downloading Turkish Linux (Pardus) because I had heard they natively supported the APU platform. HOWEVER, I was extremely delighted when I installed Ubuntu 12.10 and I was able to boot and reboot and watch my movies and edit my graphics files with no issues - with the occasional (very occasional) bug or purple-screen-boot. The 12.1 release works well, because evidently the Linux Kernel that it is packaged with supports the new AMD APU platform. Now, if I can just get catalyst control center installed I'd be overjoyed! And one final note - if building a system, make sure that you enable in your BIOS (and write to CMOS) that the board access the on-board graphics before a discrete graphics card. Otherwise it'll be black screen no matter what OS you're using!<br /><br />Linux Update - OpenSuse 12.2<br /><br />I had a few bugs with Ubuntu - not many - and switched to OpenSuse which has the KDE desktop (as opposed to Unity). I am using OpenSuse v12.2 now, and it works flawlessly, although I was more accustomed to the Ubuntu interface. I noticed that in general OpenSuse runs more smoothly than the Ubuntu release - and I was encouraged before downloading this new distro when - of all the distro webpages - I saw AMD's logo ONLY on the Opensuse webpage. So that's my take. I have noticed that almost daily the Linux and Xorg communities are getting up to speed with the AMD APU platform, and considering that the Raspberry PI SOIC can run on Linux there is no reason to need a discrete graphics card to have to get a quality GUI and distro to run on an APU based system. B00003CXBH|5.0|This is one of the best movies ever made about religion, going far beyond the history lessons and stitled hagiography of many church films, but also transcending the confused humanism and skepticism of many pseudo-sophisticated mainstream films on Christian subjects. Themes of redemption and sacrificial love are at the core of this movie's deep insights into the human person. This film also captures with pitch-perfect sensitivity the Jesuit missionary spirit -- that combination of radical availability, zeal for sharing Christ's love, and willingness to make sacrifices -- that made the Society of Jesus the unshakable and oft-mistrusted bulwark of Catholicism in the post-Reformation era. Several Jesuit priests consulted on the film (including two, Tony Lawn and Daniel Berrigan, who appear as priests in supporting roles in the film itself) and their influence is unmistakable. When you watch this film, there will be no mistaking that this is the religious order of Pope Francis. As Hilaire Belloc once said, if you can hear mass being celebrated in many parts of the world today, it is because the Jesuits gave their lives there. You can feel the spirit of St. Ignatius Loyola lurking behind every frame of this film, moving the viewer to tears of gratitude. This is the rare sort of movie that simultaneously challenges your faith and makes you proud to be a Catholic. From a technical standpoint, the filmmaking may be a bit odd and disjointed in places, but the emotional core of the actors -- along with the astounding visuals and music -- pulls you through and makes everything hold together somehow. Watch it and see.

In the Northern California town of Charming (really), a weak police force has a tacit arrangement with the \\"motorcycle club\\" The Sons of Anarchy, also known as Samcro (Sons of Anarchy Motorcycle Club, Redwood Original). The cops look the other way on Samcro's gun running and occasional internecine violence. In return, Samcro keeps drugs and other gangs out of Charming.<br /><br />The plot intricasies are far more complex, however, and herein the episodes become addictive. B005LAII08|2.0|Forcing a young woman into marriage when she is not ready does not make her happy and is generally agreed upon, outside a few uber-religious communities, as not the right thing to do.  The latest Disney heroine to take on the concept is Merida, protagonist of \\"Brave,\\" a rebellious redhead with a prowess in archery as a result of a gift bestowed upon her by a boisterous but loving father.  This is much to the dismay of her mother who wishes her to take on what many today regard as an antiquated feminine role of passivity and elegance.  The process with which she must choose her husband involves a competition among her suitors in an activity of her choice, which turns out to be--you guessed it--archery.  Needless to say, Merida, in a toast to girl power, shows up her three mediocre, socially-inept suitors with her own archery skills and proceeds to address her overall marital issue through a spell that changes the one forcing the marriage issue, her mother, into a bear.  This provides for some intense moments as Merida is forced to defend and care for her mother in light of her father who is determined to avenge a lost limb by killing the bear.<br /><br />The problems with this story are twofold:  First, fighting forced marriage is bit like fighting the Holy Roman Empire.  It is a noble fight but the enemy really doesn't exist anymore, outside a few cloistered segments of society, with whom most of the audience probably doesn't associate anyways.  Plus, Jasmine and Mulan already fought that battle, succeeded, and had presumably brought even the most obstinate detractors onboard.  Second, it is unclear exactly how showing up her suitors or turning her mother into a bear helped to win her case.  She proved that she had better aim than her suitors, but so what?  Many a successful husband has been landed without the foggiest notion on how to operate a bow-and-arrow.  Then, the bear bit showed that she could take care of her mother in spite of an army of adversaries led bynone other than her father.  But again, so what?  If the mother's motivation for a forced marriage was the security of the kingdom, would such an antic really eliminate that notion?<br /><br />The final product feels like a rush job, where several individuals of varying interests were summoned to assemble a product to meet various business objectives, with the story secondary.  The product should be the first Pixar to feature a female lead to tap into the young, soccer-playing girls who find it gratifying to show up their male counterparts.  Check.  It should have some fights so that the young male audience doesn't perceive it as a \\"girl's movie\\" and avoid it.  Check.  It should showcase Pixar's animation to be a cut above anything produced elsewhere.  Check.  It should include some magic or witchcraft so that it does not come across as an animated version of a Jane Austen novel.  Check.<br /><br />In spite of its splendid animation, the movie ultimately fails not because of its female protagonist or its magic.  It fails because of a flawed story concerning an overused adversary.

B0000024ZT|4.0|For almost any other artist, 5 stars. But compared with other, later masterworks, it's still a little uneven. Bruce is still searching for his 'sound.' Nevertheless, he manages to perform some timeless classics for this album. In spite of this, it's instructive to remember that this album at the time was received largely as a 'regional hit,' which is to say that it was wildly popular in the Tri-State area, but not so much elsewhere. I attribute that to the novel sound of the E street Band. Keyboard heavy, with hardly a guitar solo in sight, it was a little hard to pin down (and pigeonhole) when it first came out. The same reception would await his second effort, The Wild, the Innocent...' It would not be until Born to Run, that Bruce would break out of his regional audience. By that time, it was evident that Springsteen was exceptionally talented. How talented would not be fully realized until his next album, Darkness of the Edge of Town; and we all know the rest of the story. However, even on Greetings, you can hear the fully fleshed out Springsteen sound on certain tracks like Spirit in the Night and Growing Up. Still, there are some unsuccessful experiments here also. Not necessarily bad, exactly, but not in playing up to Springsteen's particular genius as a vocalist and song writer. Thank goodness, Bruce realized this as much as anyone, and his output steadily grew more and more impressive over succeeding albums. I don't think the poor initial sales of Greetings and The Wild were warranted. These are great albums by any measure; rather it was a problem of synchronicity: the world just was not ready for Bruce Springsteen in 1973. It came around, eventually!

Since he knew little about his fathers background, he provided the context for how his mother was raised. He associated with Blacks more than others, as he attempted to find a place for himself in a racially polarized  society.<br /><br />During Barack Obama's career as a community organizer, he performed duties that provided little monetary gain, and involved a lot of determination and tenacity. The work involved forging alliances among people and organizations in order to make society better. Although success would lead to satisfaction, it was difficult work, but something that provided good training for the political world.<br /><br />After working as a community organizer, Barack Obama attended Harvard Law School. He then began spending time with his sister from his father's side, and met other siblings. That led to a trip to visit more relatives in Kenya, in which he learned a lot about his father, and the things that led to his father living the life that he lived. B000005S0D|4.0|I wore my vinyl version to death years ago and recently picked up 'Stationary Traveller' on CD. I must admit that I'd put off doing that for years, simply because the tunes on this album are of a darker nature, focusing as they do on the then harrowing situation faced by those living in Communist governed (and policed!) East Berlin. There was also the fact that longtime Camel drummer Andy Ward, whose wonderfully light, musical feel was so compelling was gone, and the drumming here was more, well, typical of the genre. At this point Camel was down to founding guitarist/vocalist/flutist Andrew Latimer with a fast of very fine supporting players (including David Paton - Pilot, Alan Parsons, Elton, Kate Bush etc.). After all these years - and with the dismantling of the Berlin Wall - I found it easier to focus on the music and not the darkness of the topic. And what a delight! As always, Latimer's guitarring is wonderfully melodic and creative, yet tastefully understated unless he's soaring over the groove with one of his (truly) great solos. With titles like 'Vopos', 'Cloak & Dagger Man' and 'West Berlin' there is no avoiding the subject matter, which is made all the greyer by Latimer's somewhat monotone voice. But still the overall effect is highly compelling. The only thing that prevents me from giving this album a 5-star rating is the fact that it is missing that joyous ambiance I love so much with most other Camel albums. And that - subject matter aside - has much to do with the absence of former band members Ward, bassist/vocalist Richard Sinclair and keysman Peter Bardens (though the keys playing on this CD is excellent). I guess one could say that Camel had such a band personality that the loss of any one of those key players takes a bit of adjusting to. But this is still a fine CD, so don't consider that previous comment as a negative. Indeed, it was just another step in Camel's long history of change. B000006TSC|5.0|Camel are an eclectic band, to say the very least. The Prog Lite tag somehow seems the most appropriate of the lot, though possibly suggesting something diluted and watered down, which is definitely not the case here. Camel are definitely a most British band, and that - along with stellar musicianship, great (and very different) songwriting plus a musical spirit that exudes passion and at times ha-ha humour as in 'Down on the Farm' - is what has always made them such compelling listening. I'm a Jeff Beck fan who enjoys the likes of David Gilmour, early Mick Taylor and Peter Green etc., and I would put Camel guitarist Andrew Latimer in their ranks - easily! Indeed, he's more original than the latter two were in their heyday, and he can be as compellingly melodic and emotional as Beck, with a tone that is stunning. The guitar solo in 'Summer Lightning' qualifies as one of the great 'rock' highlights of all time (for me), as it eases its way over a hi-tempo dance beat with layers of vocal harmonies giving Latimer a tapestry over which to soar. Simply stunning! And there are numerous other fine moments throughout the album. Richard Sinclair's fretless basswork and highly unique vocal style (and tone) are extremely enjoyable and a nice contrast/complement to Latimer's voice. Pete Bardens (who played with Van Morrison in classic rockers Them) was always there with rich keyboard textures, while his solos and dual-riffing with Latimer were really quite unique. But one of the real aces with this band was drummer Andy Ward, whose ability to navigate odd times and various feels with such ease - and so unobtrusively - really made him an integral component of the music; no boring boom-chuck drumming here, kids, this is highly musical stuff that suggests fusion influences, though they're keep well in check. Also, FYI, Roger Waters wanted Latimer to be in his band, but apparently the pitch of his voice apparently didn't fit with the existing vocalists. 'Breathless' is - like so much Camel music - timeless. There are so many saving graces that I can't imagine ever not loving this album. True, if you're a Skynrd or Sabbath fan it may not be your music (though the couple Latimer solos here would sweep most other guitarists under the carpet and the power chorded intro to 'Down on the Farm' is a moment's worth of heaviness). I suggest you YouTube 'Summer Lightning'. Let it play all the way through. Then decide. I've loved this album since the 70s...it's a definite keeper. Also - if you can find it - 'Live On the Road 1982' (not '81 - there's one of those, too) is a storming set with David Paton (Pilot, Alan Parsons, Elton, Kate Bush etc.) in the band. And go search out 'Sasquatch' from the album 'Single Factor'. That instrumental is a ripping good example of Latimer at his best, and he's powered along by Simon Phillips (Jeff Beck, the Who, Toto etc.) kicking it up on the drums. Yes, there is much great Camel music to be had.
